🖥️ Hybrid System & Remote Access
(MacBook + Desktop)





💡 This setup is chosen to balance portability + performance rather than relying on a single powerful computer.





  
  

    
The remote connection between my MacBook and desktop works through Remote Desktop technology.

    
    
This allows me to connect from my MacBook to my Windows 11 desktop and run Blender, game engines, or heavy tasks directly using the RTX graphics card.

    
    
Additionally, with Wake-on-LAN I can power on the desktop remotely even when it’s shut down. Once it boots, I log in and continue working.

    
    
This system provides:

    
    
      
  • 💻 Portable work with the MacBook
    • 
      
  • 🖥️ High performance with the desktop
    • 
      
  • ⚡ Remote use of RTX GPU power
    • 
    

    
    
In short: I use a hybrid workflow that combines the advantages of a thin, quiet laptop with a powerful desktop.

  






  
  

    
Wake-on-LAN (WoL) is a technology that lets me power on a computer remotely over the network, even if it’s shut down or in sleep mode.

    
    
The network card stays in low-power mode and starts the computer when it receives a special signal called a “magic packet.”

    
    
For reliable operation, Ethernet (wired connection) is usually required, since most Wi-Fi cards cannot receive the signal when powered off.

    
    
In my setup:

    
    
      
  • 🔌 Desktop connected to the modem via Ethernet
    • 
      
  • 📡 Signal sent from MacBook or phone
    • 
      
  • ⚡ Computer powers on automatically
    • 
    

    
    
In short: Wake-on-LAN lets me start my computer without being physically present, and Ethernet ensures stable operation.

  






  
  

    
I prefer the MacBook + desktop combination to achieve both performance and portability.

    
    
With the MacBook Air I handle daily tasks, coding, and design quietly and on the go. For heavy workloads, I connect remotely to my desktop and use the RTX GPU power.

    
    
Advantages of this system:

    
    
      
  • 💻 Lightweight and quiet laptop experience
    • 
      
  • 🖥️ High performance on desktop (rendering, game engines)
    • 
      
  • 🔋 Long battery life on the laptop
    • 
      
  • ⚙️ Flexible and professional workflow
    • 
    

    
    
Instead of relying on one device for everything, I use each device in the area where it excels — which is more efficient for me.

    
    
In short: MacBook for portability, desktop for power — combined through Remote Desktop.

  






  
  

    
I prefer Windows 11 because it’s easy and compatible for my daily tasks and hobby programs.  
    Games, office software, and 3D design tools run more smoothly in a Windows environment.


    
I’ve tried Linux — it’s a very valuable system, especially for open-source and security.  
    However, some specialized software and games work more easily on Windows.


    
Note: macOS only runs on MacBook devices.  
    I like it too, especially the models with M3 chips — they’re high quality.  
    Plus, it integrates very easily with iPhone.

  






  
  

    
There are a few methods. One of the most well-known is using a bootable tool like Hiren’s BootCD.  
    You can install it on a USB drive and boot your computer from there to access your files — though it requires some technical experience.


    
An easier method is to remove the hard drive and connect it to another computer using an external HDD case.  
    That way, you can access your files like a regular USB drive.


    
Note: If the files are critical and you’re unsure about doing it yourself, it’s safer to contact a professional data recovery service.

  






  
  

    
You can use different benchmarking tools to measure performance:


    
    
      
  • Graphics card (GPU): 3DMark, Unigine Heaven, FurMark
    • 
      
  • Processor (CPU): Cinebench, Geekbench, CPU-Z
    • 
      
  • Disk (SSD/HDD): CrystalDiskMark
    • 
      
  • Memory (RAM): MemTest86 or Windows Memory Diagnostic
    • 
      
  • Overall system: PCMark, PassMark
    • 
    


    
Note: Benchmark results are mainly for comparison and diagnostics.  
    Real-world performance depends on software optimization and personal usage needs.

  






  
  

    
This is a very personal decision since everyone’s needs, budget, and usage are different.  
    For me, 3D design and hobby software are important, so I chose to build my own computer.


    
If you plan to run high-performance games or specialized software, building a custom system might be a better option.  
    But for everyday tasks, pre-built systems are more than enough for most people.


    
Note: I don’t recommend specific brands or models — the best choice depends on your own needs.

  






  
  

    
The most challenging parts were cable management and checking component compatibility.  
    But once I learned it, the process was really enjoyable.

  






  
  

    
Desktops are easier to upgrade and offer stronger performance.  
    They’re especially better for 3D printing tasks and gaming.

  






  
  

    
Note: These recommendations are entirely based on personal experience. The purchase decision is yours.

    
From my experience, iPhone 15 and Samsung Galaxy S23 have been useful phones; for laptops, MacBook Air M2, Dell XPS 15, and Lenovo ThinkPad series worked well. This is shared only for experience and information purposes.

  






  
  

    
Note: These recommendations are entirely based on personal experience. The purchase decision is yours.

    
From my experience, these tablets have been useful: iPad Air, Samsung Galaxy Tab S9, and Lenovo Tab P12. Every user’s needs may differ; the information here is only for experience and information sharing.

  






  
  

    
Yes, I can use Excel and other office programs at a basic to intermediate level.

    
While I don’t know every advanced formula in Excel, I’ve created small tools at work using Excel VBA.

    
For me, office programs are tools to create solutions based on specific needs.

  






  
  

    
I use Office 2019. It’s also available through a subscription with Office 365.  
    Free alternatives include LibreOffice and OpenOffice.

    
Note: Free programs are more than enough for basic tasks.

  






  
  

    It is not mandatory, but it provides a big advantage.  
    A programmer who understands hardware knows the limits of the code they write better.
  






  
  

    Not everyone.  
    A powerful system is needed according to the requirement; more than that is often wasteful.
  






  
  

    Yes.  
    Limited hardware teaches efficiency and optimization.
  






  
  

    Choosing parts only for performance instead of compatibility.  
    Without balance, a powerful system will not be stable.
  






  
  

    Because of incorrect assembly, insufficient cooling, and rushed usage.  
    Hardware requires patience.
  



	
  

  
 

    📐 CAD, Technical Drawing & Production Connection

    

  This section covers the transition points between software and physical production.


    
	

  
  

    

      OpenSCAD is an open-source modeling program based on writing code instead of drawing 3D models with a mouse.
    

    

      It is preferred for parts requiring dimensional accuracy, mechanical components, and repeatable designs. Thanks to its parametric structure, dimensions can be easily changed.
    

    

      It works more on logic and mathematics than visuals, giving the user full control in the design process.
    

  






  
  

    

      In OpenSCAD, 3D models are created using basic shapes, transformations, and logical operations.
    




union() {
  cube([20, 20, 10]);
  translate([10, 10, 10])
    cylinder(h=20, r=5);
}



    

      In this example:
      
    
        
  • cube([20, 20, 10]) → Creates a cube with specified width, depth, and height
    • 
        
  • translate → Changes the position of the object
    • 
        
  • union() → Combines multiple shapes into a single part
    • 
      

    




difference() {
  cube([30, 30, 10]);
  translate([5, 5, 0])
    cube([20, 20, 10]);
}



    

      The difference() command is used to subtract one shape from another.  
      This method is often preferred to create holes, gaps, and fittings.
    


    

      One of OpenSCAD’s strongest aspects is that, when understood correctly, it allows the creation of parametric, editable, and reusable models.  
      With small variable changes, different dimensions of the same design can be easily generated.
    

  






  
  

    

      OpenSCAD is based on defining objects by writing code instead of drawing with a mouse.  
      In this approach, dimensions, positions, and relationships are expressed numerically.
    


    

      In visual CAD programs, design usually progresses with drag-and-drop, sketch drawing, and creating shapes directly on the screen.
    


    

      OpenSCAD focuses more on logic, mathematics, and structure building.  
      This way, the same model can be easily reproduced or adapted with small parameter changes.
    


    

      Both approaches serve different needs.  
      OpenSCAD is especially preferred for repeatable, dimension-sensitive, and editable designs.
    

  






  
  

    

      Parametric design means defining the dimensions and proportions of an object through variables (parameters).
    


    

      Values such as width, height, or hole diameter can be changed individually, making it easy to produce different versions of the same design.
    


    

      This approach reduces dimensional errors in repetitive production and ensures controlled updates of the design.
    

  






  
  

    

      OpenSCAD allows designs to be defined with code and mathematical expressions.
    


    

      This structure provides advantages in projects requiring dimensional accuracy, repeatability, and parametric control.
    


    

      Especially for users with an engineering mindset, the design process becomes more predictable and controllable.
    

  






  
  

    

      Parametric thinking is an approach to problem-solving by defining variables instead of accepting a situation as fixed and unchanging.
    


    

      Just as dimensions in 3D design can be adjusted according to needs, in life conditions, people, and opportunities also vary.
    


    

      This perspective enables flexible thinking, adaptation, and the development of solutions tailored to individuals and situations instead of one-size-fits-all answers.
    

  






  
  

    

      I open DWG and other 2D drawings in different programs depending on the job.
      I usually use AutoCAD, FreeCAD, and sometimes
      LibreCAD.
    

    

      For architectural and technical drawings, my priorities are:
      accurate dimensions, readable layers, and ensuring the drawing can later be
      transferred into a 3D model or production.
    

    

      In some cases, I only use DWG/DXF files to review, take measurements, or as references;
      not every drawing has to be turned into a 3D model.
    

    

      
        Note: Program choice depends entirely on the purpose of the work and personal habits.
        This is not a comparison or recommendation; it reflects my personal usage preferences.
      
    

  






  
  

    

      This isn’t a “which is better?” question; it depends on what you want to achieve.
      2D and 3D serve different needs.
    

    
2D drawing is usually more practical when:

    
    
      
  • Clarity of dimensions, sections, and plans is important
    • 
      
  • Laser cutting, CNC, or technical documentation is required
    • 
      
  • A quick draft or reference drawing is needed
    • 
    

    
3D model becomes more useful when:

    
    
      
  • You need to see the volume and proportions of a part
    • 
      
  • Production with a 3D printer is planned
    • 
      
  • Assembly, collision checks, or visual inspection is required
    • 
    

    

      I usually start with 2D and then move to 3D if needed.
      This way, dimensions remain clear and potential errors can be spotted before production.
    

    

      
        Note: Using 2D or 3D is not a sign of skill level. The “right” choice depends entirely
        on the purpose of the work.
      
    

  






  
  

    

      Measurement errors may look small, but they can lead to chain problems during production.
      This is especially noticeable in 3D printing, laser cutting, and parts that require assembly.
    

    
    
      
  • Parts may not fit: Screw holes, slots, or edges may not align.
    • 
      
  • Assembly becomes difficult: Forcing parts together increases the risk of breaking or cracking.
    • 
      
  • Material and time are wasted: Incorrectly printed parts often need to be remade.
    • 
      
  • Hidden issues may appear: Small differences not noticed at first can cause problems during use.
    • 
    

    

      That’s why taking accurate measurements, leaving tolerances, and checking the model before production
      is just as important as printing itself.
    

    

      
        Note: Making measurement errors is not a sign of inexperience; it’s a natural part of the production process.
        What matters is noticing and learning to correct them.
      
    

  






  
  

    

      The most common mistake is to directly extrude a 2D drawing into 3D
      and assume it is ready for production.
    

    
Things often overlooked in this transition:

    
    
      
  • Thickness and depth: Lines that are fine in 2D may be too thin or weak in 3D.
    • 
      
  • Tolerances: No clearance may be left for joints or screw fittings.
    • 
      
  • Production orientation: The way the part will be printed is not considered during modeling.
    • 
      
  • Focusing on shape instead of function: The model may look nice but not be functional.
    • 
    

    

      My healthy approach: Use 2D as a reference, but rethink the 3D model with production logic in mind.
    

    

      
        Note: Moving from 2D to 3D is not a “level up,” but adapting to a different way of thinking.
      
    

  






  
  

    
Both are used for 2D production; the difference lies in purpose and workflow.


    
    
      
  • 
        LibreCAD:
    
        Suitable for technical drawings that require dimensional accuracy.  
        Works in millimeters; hole diameters, line lengths, and real measurements are precise.  
        (Laser cutting, mechanical parts, templates)
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Inkscape:
    
        Suitable for visual and free vector drawings.  
        Provides flexibility for logos, text, and decorative shapes.  
        (Works in SVG format, produces vector output for laser use)
      
    • 
    


    
In short:  
    If dimensional and technical accuracy is important, choose LibreCAD.  
    If visual design and shape freedom matter, choose Inkscape.


    
Note: Both are sufficient for laser production when used in the right context.

  






  
  

    

      The most common mistake is not accounting for the laser’s cutting width (kerf)
      and the production logic of the drawing.
    


    
This usually leads to problems such as:


    
    
      
  • Joints may not fit: Parts end up either too tight or too loose.
    • 
      
  • Line thickness overlooked: The laser does not cut with “zero thickness.”
    • 
      
  • Open contours: Open lines will not be cut by the laser.
    • 
      
  • Wrong scale: Differences between mm–cm–inch can cause serious production errors.
    • 
      
  • Material type ignored: Wood, MDF, and acrylic do not react the same way.
    • 
    


    

      For a proper laser cut, the drawing must be prepared not only visually but with
      the machine’s operation in mind.
    


    

      Note: A laser cutting drawing is closer to a production drawing than to graphic design.
    

  






  
  

    
Laser machines do not perform layered production; they only work on the surface.  
    That’s why 2D drawings are used as the basis.


    
    
      
  • 
        Laser cutting:
    
        Operates along the X and Y axes.  
        Follows the line, cuts, or engraves.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        3D modeling:
    
        Intended for layer-by-layer production.  
        (Suitable for 3D printers)
      
    • 
    


    
Therefore, in laser cutting:

    
    
      
  • 2D vector files such as DXF / SVG are used
    • 
      
  • Thickness, laser power, and number of passes are set by machine parameters
    • 
      
  • The path of the line matters, not the model’s height
    • 
    


    
In summary:  
    3D printers produce volume,  
    laser machines work on surfaces.  
    That’s why 2D drawings are more accurate and practical for laser cutting.

  






  
  

    
The reason DXF files appear faulty on a laser is usually not the drawing itself, but the preparation and transfer process.


    
    
      
  • 
        Scale problem:
    
        The DXF may be interpreted in inches instead of millimeters.  
        In this case, the drawing comes out much larger or smaller than intended.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Open (disconnected) lines:
    
        The laser cannot fully follow lines that are not closed; the cut may remain incomplete.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Overlapping lines:
    
        The same path is cut twice, causing burns or overly deep cuts.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Layer incompatibility:
    
        Some laser software may ignore certain layers or process them incorrectly.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Software incompatibility:
    
        Not every DXF is interpreted the same way by every laser program.
      
    • 
    


    
Therefore:  
    Always preview the DXF file in the laser software before starting the cut.


    
Note: This does not mean user error; different software can interpret DXF differently.

  






  
  

    
Even if the same DXF or SVG file is used, results can vary from machine to machine.  
    The reason is not the drawing, but differences in machine and settings.


    
    
      
  • 
        Laser power:
    
        5W, 10W, or CO₂ lasers cut the same line at different depths.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Focus adjustment:
    
        If the focus is incorrect, the cut may be weak or burnt.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Speed and number of passes:
    
        The same drawing produces different results at different speeds.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Material differences:
    
        Even similar-looking wood can vary in density.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Machine tolerance:
    
        Belt tension, mechanical play, and firmware settings affect the outcome.
      
    • 
    


    
In summary:  
    There is no “one correct setting” in laser cutting.  
    The same drawing must be adjusted with small tests on each machine.


    
That’s why the laser production process progresses through trial–observation–adjustment.

  



	
	

  
  

    
The reason small test pieces are cut in laser cutting is that  
    the correct result cannot be guaranteed in a single attempt.


    
Even with the same drawing and settings, results may vary.  
    That’s why small trials are made before the actual cut.


    
    
      
  • 
        To check power and speed settings:
    
        Too much power may burn, too little power may not complete the cut.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        To understand material differences:
    
        Even the same type of wood or acrylic can vary in density.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        To check focus:
    
        If focus is incorrect, the cut surface may be rough or incomplete.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        To detect drawing errors early:
    
        Open lines, overlapping paths, or scale issues show up in the test.
      
    • 
    


    
In summary:  
    Test cuts are not a waste of time,  
    but a safety step that protects the material and the project.


    
Note: This approach is not amateurish; on the contrary, it is part of controlled production.

  






  
  

    
Tests before laser cutting are necessary to ensure safety and achieve the correct result.


    
These tests are usually done on small, unimportant pieces or scrap material.


    
    
      
  • 
        Start with low power:
    
        In the first test, the laser runs at low power (for example, engraving power).  
        This is a safe step for checking focus and direction.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Do a position test:
    
        Check if the laser is working on the correct area of the material.  
        This reduces the risk of going off the bed or starting in the wrong place.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Try speed–power balance:
    
        The same line is tested at different speeds and powers to find the cleanest cut.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Check focus height:
    
        If the laser focus is too close or too far from the material, cut quality drops significantly.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Observe G-code behavior:
    
        Watch when the laser turns on and off (M3 / M5) and the movement order.
      
    • 
    


    
In summary:  
    Laser tests are like a controlled rehearsal before the actual cut.


    
Note: Starting the cut without testing can damage both the material and the machine.

  






  
  

    
3D is the abbreviation of the English term “Three Dimensional”.  
    In Turkish, it means “üç boyutlu” (three-dimensional).


    
A 3D object has three basic measurements:

    
    
      
  • Width (X)
    • 
      
  • Depth (Y)
    • 
      
  • Height (Z)
    • 
    


    
Examples from daily life:

    
    
      
  • A drawing on paper → 2D
    • 
      
  • An object you can hold → 3D
    • 
    


    

      3D modeling allows designing objects that can be produced in real life  
      using these three dimensions in a computer environment.
    


    

      3D printers transform these digital 3D models into real objects layer by layer.
    


    
In summary:

      3D = a digital object with measurable dimensions, volume, and the ability to be manufactured.
    

  






  
  

    

      Assembly in FreeCAD allows individually designed parts to be brought together  
      so you can see how they work in advance.  
      The goal is not just drawing, but understanding whether the parts  
      actually fit together.
    


    

      With Assembly you can:
    

    
    
      
  • Check if parts overlap or collide
    • 
      
  • See the alignment of screws, holes, and connection points
    • 
      
  • Test simple movements of parts (hinges, sliders, etc.)
    • 
      
  • Detect measurement and placement errors before production
    • 
    


    

      This feature is especially useful for  
      3D printer parts,  
      fan brackets,  
      sensor holders, and  
      modular designs.
    


    

      Important note:

      FreeCAD Assembly is not an industrial-level physics or load simulation.  
      The purpose is to see part compatibility and  
      catch possible errors early before production.
    


    

      In short, Assembly seeks to answer not “Does this part look nice?”  
      but rather “Does this actually work?”.
    

  






  
  

    

      Assembly is a powerful tool, but it is not mandatory for every project.  
      Especially for single parts or simple geometries,  
      the assembly stage may add unnecessary complexity.
    


    

      Assembly is usually required in cases such as:
    

    
    
      
  • Designs where multiple parts work together
    • 
      
  • Connections involving screws, hinges, sliders, etc.
    • 
      
  • Situations where spacing and alignment between parts are critical
    • 
    


    

      However, for:
    

    
    
      
  • Single-part 3D prints
    • 
      
  • Simple tools or brackets
    • 
      
  • Clear measurements with no moving components
    • 
    


    

      it is possible to get good results without using Assembly.
    


    

      In summary:

      Assembly is not a requirement, but a tool used when needed.  
      The goal is not to complicate the process, but to detect errors early.
    

  






  
  

    

      FEM (Finite Element Method) is an analysis method used to approximately see  
      how a part might behave under certain loads.
    


    

      In FreeCAD, FEM allows you to:
    

    
    
      
  • Apply force to a part
    • 
      
  • Define fixing points
    • 
      
  • Visualize stress distribution
    • 
    


    

      The analysis results are usually shown with colors:
    

    
    
      
  • Red areas: Regions with high stress
    • 
      
  • Green / blue areas: Regions under less stress
    • 
    


    

      These visuals help identify where the part may be more prone to breaking.  
      However, these results are not exact strength values.
    


    

      Important limitation:

      FreeCAD FEM results do not replace engineering approval, because:
    

    
    
      
  • Actual print orientation and layer structure may not be considered
    • 
      
  • Material values are generalized
    • 
      
  • Dynamic, impact, or long-term fatigue effects are limited
    • 
    


    

      In summary:

      FEM is not used to say “This part will 100% hold,”  
      but to spot weak points in advance.  
      It is especially helpful for improving designs of 3D printed parts.
    

  






  
  

    

      FEM analyses usually work with the assumption of a  
      homogeneous, single-piece material.  
      However, parts produced with a 3D printer do not fully match this assumption.
    


    

      The main reasons are:
    

    
    
      
  • 3D printed parts are produced layer by layer
    • 
      
  • Layer adhesion may not be as strong as a single-piece material
    • 
      
  • Infill percentage changes the actual internal structure
    • 
      
  • Nozzle temperature and print settings affect strength
    • 
    


    

      In FEM analysis:
    

    
    
      
  • Material is usually assumed to be uniform
    • 
      
  • Layer structure is often not considered
    • 
      
  • Print orientation and production errors are not simulated
    • 
    


    

      Therefore, FEM results show the real-life strength of a part only  
      approximately.  
      They do not provide a definitive result or guarantee.
    


    

      In summary:

      For 3D printed parts, FEM is used not to give exact results,  
      but to spot weak points in advance.
    

  






  
  

    

      In 3D printing, the print orientation of a part  
      directly affects its strength, because layers do not have the same strength in every direction.
    


    

      For example:
    

    
    
      
  • Loads parallel to layers are usually carried better
    • 
      
  • Loads across layers (Z-axis) may be weaker
    • 
      
  • The same model can break differently when printed in different orientations
    • 
    


    

      FEM analyses often do not fully model this orientation difference.  
      The analysis treats the part as if it were a single solid piece.
    


    

      Therefore FEM results:
    

    
    
      
  • May behave differently when print orientation changes
    • 
      
  • The actual breaking point may deviate from the analysis
    • 
      
  • Can be misleading especially for thin and long parts
    • 
    


    

      In summary:

      Print orientation is a critical but limitedly modeled factor  
      that directly affects FEM results in 3D printed parts.
    

  






  
  

    

      For parts produced with a 3D printer, there are more practical and realistic criteria  
      to check before or instead of FEM analysis.
    


    

      The most important ones include:
    


    
    
      
  • 
        Print orientation:
    
        Does the orientation of the layers match the direction of the load?  
        If the part takes load along the Z-axis, the risk of breaking increases.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Wall thickness (wall/perimeter):
    
        Most of the strength comes from the number of outer walls, not the infill.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Infill type and percentage:
    
        100% infill is not always the strongest solution.  
        A suitable infill pattern for the part is more important.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Fillet and corner design:
    
        Sharp corners create stress concentration.  
        Rounded transitions are often more effective than FEM corrections.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Material choice:
    
        PLA, PETG, ABS, or Nylon each show different flexibility and breaking behavior.
      
    • 
    


    

      In addition, simple physical tests are very valuable:
    

    
    
      
  • Printing the same part in different orientations and performing break tests
    • 
      
  • Visually inspecting weak points
    • 
      
  • Observing part behavior in real use
    • 
    


    

      In summary:

      For 3D printed parts, strength is determined more by  
      design decisions and print settings than by complex simulations.
    


    

      FEM analysis is not a replacement for this process;  
      it is only a supporting tool to provide insight.
    

  






  
  

    

      FEM (Finite Element Analysis) provides meaningful results especially for parts that  
      work under load, have complex geometry, and require critical tolerances.
    


    

      Situations where it is genuinely useful:
    


    
    
      
  • Metal or composite parts
    • 
      
  • Mechanical elements working under constant load or defined forces
    • 
      
  • Parts designed for industrial production
    • 
      
  • Systems where material properties are clearly known
    • 
    


    

      For hobby parts produced with a 3D printer, however,  
      the layer structure, print orientation, and production variables  
      mean FEM results do not directly translate to real life.
    


    

      Therefore, in hobby production FEM is not a tool for absolute accuracy,  
      but only a tool to provide approximate insight.
    

  






  
  

    

      Hobby production and engineering production may use the same tools,  
      but their purposes and responsibilities are different.
    


    
    
      
  • 
        Hobby production:
    
        Focused on experimentation, learning, personal needs, and creativity.  
        Errors are tolerated, and the process is educational.
      
    • 

      
  • 
        Engineering production:
    
        Based on standards, safety factors, test reports, and legal responsibility.
      
    • 
    


    

      In engineering production, calculations are documented, tests are repeated,  
      and results are binding.
    


    

      In hobby production, the goal is not a perfect result,  
      but to understand the process.
    

  






  
  

    Because in production there is no interpretation.  
    If the measurement is not clear, the part either won’t exist or will be wrong.  
    In technical drawing, measurement is the final word.
  






  
  

    Because CAD is not just drawing, it is a way of thinking.  
    Mistakes usually appear not on the screen, but in production.
  






  
  

    Design is about “what could be,” while production is about “what must be.”  
    Something that looks good on screen is not always manufacturable.
  






  
  

    Material, tolerance, machine, and human factors come into play.  
    The digital environment forgives mistakes, the real world does not.
  






  
  

    At the very beginning.  
    A design that cannot be manufactured is incomplete, no matter how beautiful it is.
  




  


   

    🚫 Commercial Requests & Boundaries
    
	

  
  

    
Note: This answer is entirely based on my personal preferences.

    
No. Building my own system was my choice, but I don’t want to take responsibility for assembling systems for others. Buying a ready‑made computer is safer in terms of warranty and smooth usage. 
      That’s why I only share ready‑made system recommendations.

  






  
  

    
Theoretically, it interests me, but right now this type of work is neither my goal nor my priority.

    
For me, programming is an area of step-by-step learning, experimentation, and specialized development.

  






  
  

    Because every custom request requires serious time and responsibility.  
    Instead of taking on every project, I prefer to choose the right ones.
  






  
  

    Because “just taking a look” usually doesn’t stay short.  
    The responsibility of the problem comes along with it.
  






  
  

    Knowledge provides guidance.  
    Free work requires time, effort, and responsibility.  
    These two are not the same thing.
  






  
  

    Because not every request progresses in a healthy way.  
    Saying no is sometimes better for both sides, including myself.

  🛠️ D – 3D Design and Production
  
    My experiences with 3D modeling, production processes, and setting up my own printer.
    
Answers are based on my personal experiences.
  





  ⚠️ Technical notes and safety warnings

  

    🧰 3D Printer Troubleshooting:

    Covers issues such as layer shifting, nozzle clogging, and filament problems.
